<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_insipidus" title="Diabetes insipidus">Diabetes insipidus</a>.</div>

<p><b>Diabetes</b>, also known as <b>diabetes mellitus</b>, is a group of <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_disorder" title="Metabolic disorder">metabolic disorders</a> characterized by a <a href="/wiki/Hyperglycemia" title="Hyperglycemia">high blood sugar</a> level (<a href="/wiki/Hyperglycemia" title="Hyperglycemia">hyperglycemia</a>) over a prolonged period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msd_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-msd-13">[13]</a></sup> Symptoms often include <a href="/wiki/Frequent_urination" title="Frequent urination">frequent urination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polydipsia" title="Polydipsia">increased thirst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyphagia" title="Polyphagia">increased appetite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> If left untreated, diabetes can cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complications_of_diabetes_mellitus" title="Complications of diabetes mellitus">many health complications</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Acute_(medicine)" title="Acute (medicine)">Acute</a> complications can include <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_ketoacidosis" title="Diabetic ketoacidosis">diabetic ketoacidosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperosmolar_hyperglycemic_state" title="Hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state">hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state</a>, or death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADA2009_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ADA2009-3">[3]</a></sup> Serious long-term complications include <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_nephropathy" title="Diabetic nephropathy">chronic kidney disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_foot_ulcer" title="Diabetic foot ulcer">foot ulcers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_neuropathy" title="Diabetic neuropathy">damage to the nerves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_retinopathy" title="Diabetic retinopathy">damage to the eyes</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_impairment" title="Cognitive impairment">cognitive impairment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sa2016_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sa2016-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diabetes is due to either the <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a> not producing enough <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, or the cells of the body not responding properly to the insulin produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green2011_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green2011-14">[14]</a></sup> Insulin is a hormone which is responsible for helping <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> from food get into cells to be used for energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> There are three main types of diabetes mellitus:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Type_1_diabetes" title="Type 1 diabetes">Type 1 diabetes</a> results from failure of the pancreas to produce enough insulin due to loss of <a href="/wiki/Beta_cell" title="Beta cell">beta cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> This form was previously referred to as "insulin-dependent diabetes mellitus" or "juvenile diabetes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> The loss of beta cells is caused by an <a href="/wiki/Autoimmunity" title="Autoimmunity">autoimmune</a> response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The cause of this autoimmune response is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> Although Type 1 diabetes usually appears during childhood or adolescence, it can also develop in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">Type 2 diabetes</a> begins with <a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a>, a condition in which cells fail to respond to insulin properly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> As the disease progresses, a lack of insulin may also develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> This form was previously referred to as "non insulin-dependent diabetes mellitus" or "adult-onset diabetes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-14"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> Type 2 diabetes is more common in older adults, but a significant increase in the prevalence of obesity among children has led to more cases of type 2 diabetes in younger people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The most common cause is a combination of <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">excessive body weight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">insufficient exercise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-15"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gestational_diabetes" title="Gestational diabetes">Gestational diabetes</a> is the third main form, and occurs when <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnant women</a> without a previous history of diabetes develop high blood sugar levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-16"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> In women with gestational diabetes, blood sugar usually returns to normal soon after delivery. However, women who had gestational diabetes during pregnancy have a higher risk of developing type 2 diabetes later in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Type 1 diabetes must be managed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insulin_injection_(soluble)" title="Insulin injection (soluble)">insulin injections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-17"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> Prevention and treatment of type 2 diabetes involves maintaining a <a href="/wiki/Healthy_diet" title="Healthy diet">healthy diet</a>, regular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">physical exercise</a>, a normal body weight, and avoiding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tobacco_use" title="Tobacco use">use of tobacco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-18"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> Type 2 diabetes may be treated with oral antidiabetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_antidiabetic" title="Oral antidiabetic">medications</a>, with or without insulin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013Top10_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013Top10-21">[21]</a></sup> Control of <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> and maintaining proper foot and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eye_care" title="Eye care">eye care</a> are important for people with the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-19"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> Insulin and some oral medications can cause <a href="/wiki/Hypoglycemia" title="Hypoglycemia">low blood sugar (hypoglycemia)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bariatric_surgery" title="Bariatric surgery">Weight loss surgery</a> in those with <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> is sometimes an effective measure in those with type 2 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Picot2009_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Picot2009-23">[23]</a></sup> Gestational diabetes usually resolves after the birth of the baby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, an estimated 463 million people had diabetes worldwide (8.8% of the adult population), with type 2 diabetes making up about 90% of the cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2019_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2019-11">[11]</a></sup> Rates are similar in women and men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vos2012_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vos2012-25">[25]</a></sup> Trends suggest that rates will continue to rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2019_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2019-11">[11]</a></sup> Diabetes at least doubles a person's risk of early death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-20"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2019, diabetes resulted in approximately 4.2 million deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2019_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2019-11">[11]</a></sup> It is the 7th leading cause of death globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The global <a href="/wiki/Economic_cost" title="Economic cost">economic cost</a> of diabetes-related health expenditure in 2017 was estimated at <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$727</span> billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2019_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2019-11">[11]</a></sup> In the United States, diabetes cost nearly US$327 billion in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Average medical expenditures among people with diabetes are about 2.3 times higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Main_symptoms_of_diabetes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="1029" decoding="async" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Main_symptoms_of_diabetes.svg/330px-Main_symptoms_of_diabetes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Main_symptoms_of_diabetes.svg/495px-Main_symptoms_of_diabetes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Main_symptoms_of_diabetes.svg/660px-Main_symptoms_of_diabetes.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Main_symptoms_of_diabetes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview of the most significant symptoms of diabetes</div></div></div>
<p>The classic symptoms of untreated diabetes are unintended <a href="/wiki/Weight_loss" title="Weight loss">weight loss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyuria" title="Polyuria">polyuria</a> (increased urination), <a href="/wiki/Polydipsia" title="Polydipsia">polydipsia</a> (increased thirst), and <a href="/wiki/Polyphagia" title="Polyphagia">polyphagia</a> (increased hunger).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Symptoms may develop rapidly (weeks or months) in type 1 diabetes, while they usually develop much more slowly and may be subtle or absent in type 2 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2019Fact_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2019Fact-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several other signs and symptoms can mark the onset of diabetes although they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symptom#Types" title="Symptom">not specific</a> to the disease. In addition to the known symptoms listed above, they include <a href="/wiki/Blurred_vision" title="Blurred vision">blurred vision</a>, <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">fatigue</a>, slow <a href="/wiki/Wound_healing" title="Wound healing">healing of cuts</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pruritus" title="Pruritus">itchy skin</a>. Prolonged high blood glucose can cause glucose absorption in the <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">lens of the eye</a>, which leads to changes in its shape, resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_changes" title="Vision changes">vision changes</a>. Long-term vision loss can also be caused by <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_retinopathy" title="Diabetic retinopathy">diabetic retinopathy</a>. A number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_rashes" title="Skin rashes">skin rashes</a> that can occur in diabetes are collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_dermadrome" title="Diabetic dermadrome">diabetic dermadromes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diabetic_emergencies">Diabetic emergencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Diabetic emergencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>People with diabetes (usually but not exclusively in type 1 diabetes) may also experience <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_ketoacidosis" title="Diabetic ketoacidosis">diabetic ketoacidosis</a> (DKA), a metabolic disturbance characterized by nausea, vomiting and <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">abdominal pain</a>, the smell of <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a> on the breath, deep breathing known as <a href="/wiki/Kussmaul_breathing" title="Kussmaul breathing">Kussmaul breathing</a>, and in severe cases a decreased level of consciousness. DKA requires emergency treatment in hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADA2009_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-ADA2009-3">[3]</a></sup> A rarer but more dangerous condition is <a href="/wiki/Hyperosmolar_hyperglycemic_state" title="Hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state">hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state</a> (HHS), which is more common in type 2 diabetes and is mainly the result of <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> caused by high blood sugars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADA2009_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-ADA2009-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Treatment-related <a href="/wiki/Hypoglycemia" title="Hypoglycemia">low blood sugar</a> (hypoglycemia) is common in people with type 1 and also type 2 diabetes depending on the medication being used. Most cases are mild and are not considered <a href="/wiki/Medical_emergency" title="Medical emergency">medical emergencies</a>. Effects can range from <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">feelings of unease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perspiration" title="Perspiration">sweating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tremor" title="Tremor">trembling</a>, and increased appetite in mild cases to more serious effects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altered_mental_status" title="Altered mental status">confusion</a>, changes in behavior such as <a href="/wiki/Aggression" title="Aggression">aggressiveness</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epileptic_seizure" title="Epileptic seizure">seizures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unconsciousness" title="Unconsciousness">unconsciousness</a>, and rarely permanent <a href="/wiki/Brain_damage" title="Brain damage">brain damage</a> or <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a> in severe cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenny2014_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenny2014-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verrotti2012_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verrotti2012-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tachypnea" title="Tachypnea">Rapid breathing</a>, sweating, and cold, pale skin are characteristic of low blood sugar but not definitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Mild to moderate cases are self-treated by eating or drinking something high in rapidly absorbed carbohydrates. Severe cases can lead to unconsciousness and must be treated with intravenous glucose or <a href="/wiki/Glucagon_rescue" title="Glucagon rescue">injections with glucagon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complications">Complications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Complications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Complications_of_diabetes" title="Complications of diabetes">Complications of diabetes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diabetes_complications.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Diabetes_complications.jpg/220px-Diabetes_complications.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Diabetes_complications.jpg/330px-Diabetes_complications.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Diabetes_complications.jpg/440px-Diabetes_complications.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diabetes_complications.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Retinopathy, nephropathy, and neuropathy are potential complications of diabetes</div></div></div>
<p>All forms of diabetes increase the risk of long-term complications. These typically develop after many years (10–20) but may be the first symptom in those who have otherwise not received a diagnosis before that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The major long-term complications relate to damage to <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>. Diabetes doubles the risk of <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and about 75% of deaths in people with diabetes are due to <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">coronary artery disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Macrovascular_disease" title="Macrovascular disease">macrovascular diseases</a> include <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_artery_disease" title="Peripheral artery disease">peripheral artery disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> These complications are also a strong risk factor for severe <a href="/wiki/COVID-19" title="COVID-19">COVID-19</a> illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary complications of diabetes due to damage in small blood vessels include damage to the eyes, kidneys, and nerves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_DMComp_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_DMComp-42">[42]</a></sup> Damage to the eyes, known as <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_retinopathy" title="Diabetic retinopathy">diabetic retinopathy</a>, is caused by damage to the blood vessels in the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> of the eye, and can result in gradual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_loss" title="Vision loss">vision loss</a> and eventual <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">blindness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_DMComp_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_DMComp-42">[42]</a></sup> Diabetes also increases the risk of having <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma" title="Glaucoma">glaucoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataracts</a>, and other eye problems. It is recommended that people with diabetes visit an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophthalmologist" title="Ophthalmologist">eye doctor</a> once a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Damage to the kidneys, known as <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_nephropathy" title="Diabetic nephropathy">diabetic nephropathy</a>, can lead to <a href="/wiki/Glomerulosclerosis" title="Glomerulosclerosis">tissue scarring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proteinuria" title="Proteinuria">urine protein loss</a>, and eventually <a href="/wiki/Chronic_kidney_disease" title="Chronic kidney disease">chronic kidney disease</a>, sometimes requiring <a href="/wiki/Kidney_dialysis" title="Kidney dialysis">dialysis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kidney_transplantation" title="Kidney transplantation">kidney transplantation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_DMComp_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_DMComp-42">[42]</a></sup> Damage to the nerves of the body, known as <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_neuropathy" title="Diabetic neuropathy">diabetic neuropathy</a>, is the most common complication of diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_DMComp_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_DMComp-42">[42]</a></sup> The symptoms can include <a href="/wiki/Hypoesthesia" title="Hypoesthesia">numbness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paresthesia" title="Paresthesia">tingling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudomotor" title="Sudomotor">sudomotor dysfunction</a>, pain, and altered pain sensation, which can lead to damage to the skin. <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_foot" title="Diabetic foot">Diabetes-related foot problems</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_foot_ulcer" title="Diabetic foot ulcer">diabetic foot ulcers</a>) may occur, and can be difficult to treat, occasionally requiring <a href="/wiki/Amputation" title="Amputation">amputation</a>. Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Proximal_diabetic_neuropathy" title="Proximal diabetic neuropathy">proximal diabetic neuropathy</a> causes painful <a href="/wiki/Muscle_atrophy" title="Muscle atrophy">muscle atrophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muscle_weakness" title="Muscle weakness">weakness</a>.
</p><p>There is a link between <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_deficit" title="Cognitive deficit">cognitive deficit</a> and diabetes. Compared to those without diabetes, those with the disease have a 1.2 to 1.5-fold greater rate of decline in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_function" title="Cognitive function">cognitive function</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cognitive_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-cognitive-44">[44]</a></sup> Having diabetes, especially when on insulin, increases the risk of falls in older people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin:10px">
<caption>Comparison of type 1 and 2 diabetes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2011_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Will2011-46">[46]</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Feature</th>
<th>Type 1 diabetes</th>

<td>~6% (men), ~5% (women)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Diabetes mellitus is classified into six categories: <a href="/wiki/Type_1_diabetes" title="Type 1 diabetes">type 1 diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>, hybrid forms of diabetes, hyperglycemia first detected during pregnancy, "unclassified diabetes", and "other specific types".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> "Hybrid forms of diabetes" include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latent_autoimmune_diabetes_of_adults" title="Latent autoimmune diabetes of adults">slowly evolving, immune-mediated diabetes of adults</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ketosis-prone_diabetes" title="Ketosis-prone diabetes">ketosis-prone type 2 diabetes</a>. "Hyperglycemia first detected during pregnancy" includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestational_diabetes_mellitus" title="Gestational diabetes mellitus">gestational diabetes mellitus</a> and diabetes mellitus in pregnancy (type 1 or type 2 diabetes first diagnosed during pregnancy). The "other specific types" are a collection of a few dozen individual causes. Diabetes is a more variable disease than once thought and people may have combinations of forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuomi2014_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tuomi2014-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_1">Type 1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Type 1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Type_1_diabetes" title="Type 1 diabetes">Type 1 diabetes</a></div>
<p>Type 1 diabetes is characterized by loss of the insulin-producing <a href="/wiki/Beta_cell" title="Beta cell">beta cells</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_islets" title="Pancreatic islets">pancreatic islets</a>, leading to insulin deficiency. This type can be further classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immune-mediated" title="Immune-mediated">immune-mediated</a> or <a href="/wiki/Idiopathic_disease" title="Idiopathic disease">idiopathic</a>. The majority of type 1 diabetes is of an immune-mediated nature, in which a <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cell</a>-mediated <a href="/wiki/Autoimmunity" title="Autoimmunity">autoimmune</a> attack leads to the loss of beta cells and thus insulin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rother_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rother-57">[57]</a></sup> It causes approximately 10% of diabetes mellitus cases in North America and Europe. Most affected people are otherwise healthy and of a healthy weight when onset occurs. Sensitivity and responsiveness to insulin are usually normal, especially in the early stages. Although it has been called "juvenile diabetes" due to the frequent onset in children, the majority of individuals living with type 1 diabetes are now adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Change2014_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Change2014-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Brittle" diabetes, also known as unstable diabetes or labile diabetes, is a term that was traditionally used to describe the dramatic and recurrent swings in <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> levels, often occurring for no apparent reason in insulin-dependent diabetes. This term, however, has no biologic basis and should not be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merck1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-merck1-58">[58]</a></sup> Still, type 1 diabetes can be accompanied by irregular and unpredictable high blood sugar levels, and the potential for <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_ketoacidosis" title="Diabetic ketoacidosis">diabetic ketoacidosis</a> or serious low blood sugar levels. Other complications include an impaired counterregulatory response to low blood sugar, infection, <a href="/wiki/Gastroparesis" title="Gastroparesis">gastroparesis</a> (which leads to erratic absorption of dietary carbohydrates), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocrinopathies" title="Endocrinopathies">endocrinopathies</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Addison%27s_disease" title="Addison's disease">Addison's disease</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merck1_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-merck1-58">[58]</a></sup> These phenomena are believed to occur no more frequently than in 1% to 2% of persons with type 1 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid406527_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid406527-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Type_1_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Type_1_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg/220px-Type_1_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Type_1_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg/330px-Type_1_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Type_1_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg/440px-Type_1_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Type_1_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Autoimmune attack in type 1 diabetes.</div></div></div>
<p>Type 1 diabetes is partly <a href="/wiki/Genetic_disorder" title="Genetic disorder">inherited</a>, with multiple genes, including certain <a href="/wiki/Human_leukocyte_antigen" title="Human leukocyte antigen">HLA genotypes</a>, known to influence the risk of diabetes. In genetically susceptible people, the onset of diabetes can be triggered by one or more <a href="/wiki/Environmental_factor" title="Environmental factor">environmental factors</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetzoldSolimena2015_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-PetzoldSolimena2015-60">[60]</a></sup> such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viral_infection" title="Viral infection">viral infection</a> or diet. Several viruses have been implicated, but to date there is no stringent evidence to support this hypothesis in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetzoldSolimena2015_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-PetzoldSolimena2015-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ButaliaKaplan2016_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-ButaliaKaplan2016-61">[61]</a></sup> Among dietary factors, data suggest that <a href="/wiki/Gliadin" title="Gliadin">gliadin</a> (a protein present in <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>) may play a role in the development of type 1 diabetes, but the mechanism is not fully understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SerenaCamhi2015_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-SerenaCamhi2015-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VisserRozing2009_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-VisserRozing2009-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Type 1 diabetes can occur at any age, and a significant proportion is diagnosed during adulthood. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latent_autoimmune_diabetes_of_adults" title="Latent autoimmune diabetes of adults">Latent autoimmune diabetes of adults</a> (LADA) is the diagnostic term applied when type 1 diabetes develops in adults; it has a slower onset than the same condition in children. Given this difference, some use the unofficial term "type 1.5 diabetes" for this condition. Adults with LADA are frequently initially misdiagnosed as having type 2 diabetes, based on age rather than a cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_2">Type 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Type 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">Type 2 diabetes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Type_2_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Type_2_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg/220px-Type_2_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Type_2_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg/330px-Type_2_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Type_2_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg/440px-Type_2_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Type_2_Diabetes_Mellitus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reduced insulin secretion or weaker effect of insulin on its receptor leads to high glucose content in the blood.</div></div></div>
<p>Type 2 diabetes is characterized by <a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a>, which may be combined with relatively reduced insulin secretion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green2011_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Green2011-14">[14]</a></sup> The defective responsiveness of body tissues to insulin is believed to involve the <a href="/wiki/Insulin_receptor" title="Insulin receptor">insulin receptor</a>. However, the specific defects are not known. Diabetes mellitus cases due to a known defect are classified separately. Type 2 diabetes is the most common type of diabetes mellitus accounting for 95% of diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-21"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> Many people with type 2 diabetes have evidence of <a href="/wiki/Prediabetes" title="Prediabetes">prediabetes</a> (impaired fasting glucose and/or impaired glucose tolerance) before meeting the criteria for type 2 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The progression of prediabetes to overt type 2 diabetes can be slowed or reversed by lifestyle changes or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-diabetic_drug" title="Anti-diabetic drug">medications</a> that improve insulin sensitivity or reduce the <a href="/wiki/Glycogenolysis" title="Glycogenolysis">liver's glucose production</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid30528418_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30528418-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Type 2 diabetes is primarily due to lifestyle factors and genetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fat2009_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fat2009-67">[67]</a></sup> A number of lifestyle factors are known to be important to the development of type 2 diabetes, including <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> (defined by a <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> of greater than 30), lack of <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a>, poor <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stress_(biology)" title="Stress (biology)">stress</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2011_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Will2011-46">[46]</a></sup> Excess body fat is associated with 30% of cases in people of Chinese and Japanese descent, 60–80% of cases in those of European and African descent, and 100% of Pima Indians and Pacific Islanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green2011_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Green2011-14">[14]</a></sup> Even those who are not obese may have a high <a href="/wiki/Waist%E2%80%93hip_ratio" title="Waist–hip ratio">waist–hip ratio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green2011_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Green2011-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dietary factors such as <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>-sweetened drinks are associated with an increased risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SSB2010_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-SSB2010-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The type of <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> in the diet is also important, with <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trans_fat" title="Trans fat">trans fats</a> increasing the risk and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fat" title="Polyunsaturated fat">polyunsaturated</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monounsaturated_fat" title="Monounsaturated fat">monounsaturated fat</a> decreasing the risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fat2009_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fat2009-67">[67]</a></sup> Eating <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">white rice</a> excessively may increase the risk of diabetes, especially in Chinese and Japanese people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Lack of physical activity may increase the risk of diabetes in some people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22818936_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22818936-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adverse_Childhood_Experiences_Study" title="Adverse Childhood Experiences Study">Adverse childhood experiences</a>, including abuse, neglect, and household difficulties, increase the likelihood of type 2 diabetes later in life by 32%, with <a href="/wiki/Neglect" title="Neglect">neglect</a> having the strongest effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antipsychotic" title="Antipsychotic">Antipsychotic medication</a> side effects (specifically metabolic abnormalities, <a href="/wiki/Dyslipidemia" title="Dyslipidemia">dyslipidemia</a> and weight gain) and unhealthy lifestyles (including poor diet and decreased <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a>), are potential risk factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gestational_diabetes">Gestational diabetes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Gestational diabetes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gestational_diabetes" title="Gestational diabetes">Gestational diabetes</a></div>
<p>Gestational diabetes resembles type 2 diabetes in several respects, involving a combination of relatively inadequate insulin secretion and responsiveness. It occurs in about 2–10% of all <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancies</a> and may improve or disappear after delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NDIC_Stats_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-NDIC_Stats-74">[74]</a></sup> It is recommended that all pregnant women get tested starting around 24–28 weeks gestation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-75">[75]</a></sup> It is most often diagnosed in the second or third trimester because of the increase in insulin-antagonist hormone levels that occurs at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-75">[75]</a></sup> However, after pregnancy approximately 5–10% of women with gestational diabetes are found to have another form of diabetes, most commonly type 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NDIC_Stats_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-NDIC_Stats-74">[74]</a></sup> Gestational diabetes is fully treatable, but requires careful medical supervision throughout the pregnancy. Management may include dietary changes, blood glucose monitoring, and in some cases, insulin may be required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though it may be transient, untreated gestational diabetes can damage the health of the fetus or mother. Risks to the baby include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrosomia" title="Macrosomia">macrosomia</a> (high birth weight), <a href="/wiki/Congenital_heart_defect" title="Congenital heart defect">congenital heart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> abnormalities, and <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscle</a> malformations. Increased levels of insulin in a fetus's blood may inhibit fetal <a href="/wiki/Surfactant" title="Surfactant">surfactant</a> production and cause <a href="/wiki/Infant_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Infant respiratory distress syndrome">infant respiratory distress syndrome</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Bilirubin#Hyperbilirubinemia" title="Bilirubin">high blood bilirubin level</a> may result from <a href="/wiki/Hemolysis" title="Hemolysis">red blood cell destruction</a>. In severe cases, perinatal death may occur, most commonly as a result of poor placental perfusion due to vascular impairment. <a href="/wiki/Labor_induction" title="Labor induction">Labor induction</a> may be indicated with decreased placental function. A <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">caesarean section</a> may be performed if there is marked <a href="/wiki/Fetal_distress" title="Fetal distress">fetal distress</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> or an increased risk of injury associated with macrosomia, such as <a href="/wiki/Shoulder_dystocia" title="Shoulder dystocia">shoulder dystocia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_types">Other types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maturity_onset_diabetes_of_the_young" title="Maturity onset diabetes of the young">Maturity onset diabetes of the young</a> (MODY) is a rare <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(genetics)" title="Dominance (genetics)">autosomal dominant</a> inherited form of diabetes, due to one of several single-gene mutations causing defects in insulin production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> It is significantly less common than the three main types, constituting 1–2% of all cases. The name of this disease refers to early hypotheses as to its nature. Being due to a defective gene, this disease varies in age at presentation and in severity according to the specific gene defect; thus, there are at least 13 subtypes of MODY. People with MODY often can control it without using insulin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some cases of diabetes are caused by the body's tissue receptors not responding to insulin (even when insulin levels are normal, which is what separates it from type 2 diabetes); this form is very uncommon. Genetic mutations (<a href="/wiki/Dominance_(genetics)" title="Dominance (genetics)">autosomal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">mitochondrial</a>) can lead to defects in beta cell function. Abnormal insulin action may also have been genetically determined in some cases. Any disease that causes extensive damage to the pancreas may lead to diabetes (for example, <a href="/wiki/Chronic_pancreatitis" title="Chronic pancreatitis">chronic pancreatitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>). Diseases associated with excessive secretion of <a href="/wiki/Insulin_receptor" title="Insulin receptor">insulin-antagonistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> can cause diabetes (which is typically resolved once the hormone excess is removed). Many drugs impair insulin secretion and some toxins damage pancreatic beta cells, whereas others increase <a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">glucocorticoids</a> which can provoke "<a href="/wiki/Steroid_diabetes" title="Steroid diabetes">steroid diabetes</a>"). The <a href="/wiki/ICD-10" title="ICD-10">ICD-10</a> (1992) diagnostic entity, <i>malnutrition-related diabetes mellitus</i> (ICD-10 code E12), was deprecated by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) when the current taxonomy was introduced in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO1999-DefDiagClass_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO1999-DefDiagClass-81">[81]</a></sup>
Yet another form of diabetes that people may develop is double diabetes. This is when a type 1 diabetic becomes insulin resistant, the hallmark for type 2 diabetes or has a family history for type 2 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> It was first discovered in 1990 or 1991.
</p><p>The following is a list of disorders that may increase the risk of diabetes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>

<li>Drugs
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucocorticoids" title="Glucocorticoids">Glucocorticoids</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroid_hormone" title="Thyroid hormone">Thyroid hormone</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%92-adrenergic_agonist" title="Β-adrenergic agonist">β-adrenergic agonists</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statins" title="Statins">Statins</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20167359_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20167359-84">[84]</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>
</div><div class="multicol-float-clear" style=""></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suckale08_fig3_glucose_insulin_day.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Suckale08_fig3_glucose_insulin_day.png/310px-Suckale08_fig3_glucose_insulin_day.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Suckale08_fig3_glucose_insulin_day.png/465px-Suckale08_fig3_glucose_insulin_day.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Suckale08_fig3_glucose_insulin_day.png/620px-Suckale08_fig3_glucose_insulin_day.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suckale08_fig3_glucose_insulin_day.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The fluctuation of <a href="/wiki/Blood_sugar_level" title="Blood sugar level">blood sugar</a> (red) and the sugar-lowering hormone <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> (blue) in humans during the course of a day with three meals. One of the effects of a <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sugar</a>-rich vs a <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>-rich meal is highlighted.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glucose-insulin-release.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Glucose-insulin-release.svg/310px-Glucose-insulin-release.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Glucose-insulin-release.svg/465px-Glucose-insulin-release.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Glucose-insulin-release.svg/620px-Glucose-insulin-release.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glucose-insulin-release.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mechanism of insulin release in normal pancreatic <a href="/wiki/Beta_cell" title="Beta cell">beta cells</a>. Insulin production is more or less constant within the beta cells. Its release is triggered by food, chiefly food containing absorbable glucose.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">Insulin</a> is the principal hormone that regulates the uptake of <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> from the blood into most cells of the body, especially liver, adipose tissue and muscle, except smooth muscle, in which insulin acts via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IGF-1" title="IGF-1">IGF-1</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Therefore, deficiency of insulin or the insensitivity of its <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptors</a> play a central role in all forms of diabetes mellitus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The body obtains glucose from three main sources: the intestinal absorption of food; the breakdown of <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glycogenolysis" title="Glycogenolysis">glycogenolysis</a>), the storage form of glucose found in the liver; and <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a>, the generation of glucose from non-carbohydrate substrates in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenspanEndo_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-GreenspanEndo-86">[86]</a></sup> Insulin plays a critical role in regulating glucose levels in the body. Insulin can inhibit the breakdown of glycogen or the process of gluconeogenesis, it can stimulate the transport of glucose into fat and muscle cells, and it can stimulate the storage of glucose in the form of glycogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenspanEndo_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-GreenspanEndo-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insulin is released into the blood by beta cells (β-cells), found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islets_of_Langerhans" title="Islets of Langerhans">islets of Langerhans</a> in the pancreas, in response to rising levels of blood glucose, typically after eating. Insulin is used by about two-thirds of the body's cells to absorb glucose from the blood for use as fuel, for conversion to other needed molecules, or for storage. Lower glucose levels result in decreased insulin release from the beta cells and in the breakdown of glycogen to glucose. This process is mainly controlled by the hormone <a href="/wiki/Glucagon" title="Glucagon">glucagon</a>, which acts in the opposite manner to insulin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GanongsPhys_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-GanongsPhys-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>If the amount of insulin available is insufficient, or if cells respond poorly to the effects of insulin (<a href="/wiki/Insulin_resistance" title="Insulin resistance">insulin resistance</a>), or if the insulin itself is defective, then glucose is not absorbed properly by the body cells that require it, and is not stored appropriately in the liver and muscles. The net effect is persistently high levels of blood glucose, poor <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein synthesis</a>, and other metabolic derangements, such as metabolic <a href="/wiki/Acidosis" title="Acidosis">acidosis</a> in cases of complete insulin deficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenspanEndo_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-GreenspanEndo-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>When glucose concentration in the blood remains high over time, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidneys" title="Kidneys">kidneys</a> reach a threshold of <a href="/wiki/Reabsorption" title="Reabsorption">reabsorption</a>, and the body excretes glucose in the <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glycosuria" title="Glycosuria">glycosuria</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> This increases the <a href="/wiki/Osmotic_pressure" title="Osmotic pressure">osmotic pressure</a> of the urine and inhibits reabsorption of water by the kidney, resulting in increased urine production (<a href="/wiki/Polyuria" title="Polyuria">polyuria</a>) and increased fluid loss. Lost blood volume is replaced osmotically from water in body cells and other body compartments, causing <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> and increased thirst (<a href="/wiki/Polydipsia" title="Polydipsia">polydipsia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreenspanEndo_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-GreenspanEndo-86">[86]</a></sup> In addition, intracellular glucose deficiency stimulates appetite leading to excessive food intake (polyphagia).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Glycated_hemoglobin" title="Glycated hemoglobin">Glycated hemoglobin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glucose_tolerance_test" title="Glucose tolerance test">Glucose tolerance test</a></div>
<p>Diabetes mellitus is diagnosed with a test for the glucose content in the blood, and is diagnosed by demonstrating any one of the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO1999-DefDiagClass_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO1999-DefDiagClass-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasting_glucose" title="Fasting glucose">Fasting plasma glucose level</a> ≥ 7.0 mmol/L (126 mg/dL). For this test, blood is taken after a period of fasting, i.e. in the morning before breakfast, after the patient had sufficient time to fast overnight.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_glucose" title="Plasma glucose">Plasma glucose</a> ≥ 11.1 mmol/L (200 mg/dL) two hours after a 75 gram oral glucose load as in a <a href="/wiki/Glucose_tolerance_test" title="Glucose tolerance test">glucose tolerance test</a> (OGTT)</li>
<li>Symptoms of high blood sugar and plasma glucose ≥ 11.1 mmol/L (200 mg/dL) either while fasting or not fasting</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Glycated_hemoglobin" title="Glycated hemoglobin">Glycated hemoglobin</a> (HbA<sub>1C</sub>) ≥ 48 mmol/mol (≥ 6.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_control_and_complications_trial" title="Diabetes control and complications trial">DCCT</a> %).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li></ul>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left:15px; text-align:center">
