<p>A positive result, in the absence of unequivocal high blood sugar, should be confirmed by a repeat of any of the above methods on a different day. It is preferable to measure a fasting glucose level because of the ease of measurement and the considerable time commitment of formal glucose tolerance testing, which takes two hours to complete and offers no prognostic advantage over the fasting test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> According to the current definition, two fasting glucose measurements above 7.0 mmol/L (126 mg/dL) is considered diagnostic for diabetes mellitus.
</p><p>Per the WHO, people with fasting glucose levels from 6.1 to 6.9 mmol/L (110 to 125 mg/dL) are considered to have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impaired_fasting_glycemia" title="Impaired fasting glycemia">impaired fasting glucose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> People with plasma glucose at or above 7.8 mmol/L (140 mg/dL), but not over 11.1 mmol/L (200 mg/dL), two hours after a 75 gram oral glucose load are considered to have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impaired_glucose_tolerance" title="Impaired glucose tolerance">impaired glucose tolerance</a>. Of these two prediabetic states, the latter in particular is a major risk factor for progression to full-blown diabetes mellitus, as well as cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Diabetes_Association" title="American Diabetes Association">American Diabetes Association</a> (ADA) since 2003 uses a slightly different range for impaired fasting glucose of 5.6 to 6.9 mmol/L (100 to 125 mg/dL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Glycated_hemoglobin" title="Glycated hemoglobin">Glycated hemoglobin</a> is better than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasting_glucose" title="Fasting glucose">fasting glucose</a> for determining risks of cardiovascular disease and death from any cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prevention_of_type_2_diabetes" title="Prevention of type 2 diabetes">Prevention of type 2 diabetes</a></div>
<p>There is no known <a href="/wiki/Preventive_healthcare" title="Preventive healthcare">preventive</a> measure for type 1 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-22"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> Type 2 diabetes—which accounts for 85–90% of all cases worldwide—can often be prevented or delayed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> by maintaining a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_body_weight" title="Normal body weight">normal body weight</a>, engaging in physical activity, and eating a healthy diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2022_2-23"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2022-2">[2]</a></sup> Higher levels of physical activity (more than 90 minutes per day) reduce the risk of diabetes by 28%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2016_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2016-99">[99]</a></sup> Dietary changes known to be effective in helping to prevent diabetes include maintaining a diet rich in <a href="/wiki/Whole_grain" title="Whole grain">whole grains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">fiber</a>, and choosing good fats, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fat" title="Polyunsaturated fat">polyunsaturated fats</a> found in nuts, vegetable oils, and fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarvardNutrition_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-HarvardNutrition-100">[100]</a></sup> Limiting sugary beverages and eating less red meat and other sources of <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a> can also help prevent diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarvardNutrition_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-HarvardNutrition-100">[100]</a></sup> Tobacco smoking is also associated with an increased risk of diabetes and its complications, so <a href="/wiki/Smoking_cessation" title="Smoking cessation">smoking cessation</a> can be an important preventive measure as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relationship between type 2 diabetes and the main modifiable risk factors (excess weight, unhealthy diet, physical inactivity and tobacco use) is similar in all regions of the world. There is growing evidence that the underlying determinants of diabetes are a reflection of the major forces driving social, economic and cultural change: <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>, urbanization, population aging, and the general <a href="/wiki/Health_policy" title="Health policy">health policy</a> environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_management" title="Diabetes management">Diabetes management</a></div>
<p>Diabetes management concentrates on keeping blood sugar levels as close to normal, without causing low blood sugar. This can usually be accomplished with dietary changes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> exercise, weight loss, and use of appropriate medications (insulin, oral medications).
</p><p>Learning about the disease and actively participating in the treatment is important, since complications are far less common and less severe in people who have well-managed blood sugar levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Per the <a href="/wiki/American_College_of_Physicians" title="American College of Physicians">American College of Physicians</a>, the goal of treatment is an HbA<sub>1C</sub> level of 7-8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Attention is also paid to other health problems that may accelerate the negative effects of diabetes. These include <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">high blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">metabolic syndrome</a> <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, and lack of regular <a href="/wiki/Exercise" title="Exercise">exercise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE66_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICE66-107">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_shoe" title="Diabetic shoe">Specialized footwear</a> is widely used to reduce the risk of <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_foot_ulcer" title="Diabetic foot ulcer">diabetic foot ulcers</a> by relieving the pressure on the foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The principles of managing diabetes may be similar across the general population with diabetes, however some considerations may need to be addressed when tailoring intervention, mainly in special populations.
</p><p>Considering those with severe <a href="/wiki/Mental_disorder" title="Mental disorder">mental illness</a>, the efficacy of <a href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a> self-management interventions is still poorly explored, with insufficient scientific evidence to show whether these interventions have similar results to those observed in general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifestyle">Lifestyle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Lifestyle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Diet_in_diabetes" title="Diet in diabetes">Diet in diabetes</a></div>
<p>People with diabetes can benefit from education about the disease and treatment, dietary changes, and exercise, with the goal of keeping both short-term and long-term blood glucose levels <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_management#Glycemic_control" title="Diabetes management">within acceptable bounds</a>. In addition, given the associated higher risks of cardiovascular disease, lifestyle modifications are recommended to control blood pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Weight_loss" title="Weight loss">Weight loss</a> can prevent progression from prediabetes to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes type 2</a>, decrease the risk of cardiovascular disease, or result in a partial remission in people with diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADA2019_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADA2019-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADA2018_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADA2018-115">[115]</a></sup> No single dietary pattern is best for all people with diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Em2015_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Em2015-116">[116]</a></sup> Healthy dietary patterns, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_diet" title="Mediterranean diet">Mediterranean diet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Low-carbohydrate_diet" title="Low-carbohydrate diet">low-carbohydrate diet</a>, or <a href="/wiki/DASH_diet" title="DASH diet">DASH diet</a>, are often recommended, although evidence does not support one over the others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADA2019_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-ADA2019-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADA2018_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-ADA2018-115">[115]</a></sup> According to the ADA, "reducing overall carbohydrate intake for individuals with diabetes has demonstrated the most evidence for improving glycemia", and for individuals with type 2 diabetes who cannot meet the glycemic targets or where reducing anti-glycemic medications is a priority, <a href="/wiki/Low-carbohydrate_diet" title="Low-carbohydrate diet">low or very-low carbohydrate diets</a> are a viable approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADA2018_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-ADA2018-115">[115]</a></sup> For overweight people with type 2 diabetes, any diet that achieves weight loss is effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Em2015_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Em2015-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications">Medications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Medications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_medication" title="Diabetes medication">Diabetes medication</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Glucose_control">Glucose control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Glucose control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-diabetic_medication" title="Anti-diabetic medication">Anti-diabetic medication</a></div>
<p>Most medications used to treat diabetes act by lowering <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">blood sugar levels</a> through different mechanisms. There is broad consensus that when people with diabetes maintain tight glucose control – keeping the glucose levels in their blood within normal ranges – they experience fewer complications, such as <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_nephropathy" title="Diabetic nephropathy">kidney problems</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_retinopathy" title="Diabetic retinopathy">eye problems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> There is however debate as to whether this is appropriate and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cost_effective" title="Cost effective">cost effective</a> for people later in life in whom the risk of hypoglycemia may be more significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pozzilli2014_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pozzilli2014-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are a number of different classes of anti-diabetic medications. Type 1 diabetes requires treatment with <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, ideally using a "basal bolus" regimen that most closely matches normal insulin release: long-acting insulin for the <a href="/wiki/Basal_rate" title="Basal rate">basal rate</a> and short-acting insulin with meals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICENG17_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICENG17-121">[121]</a></sup> Type 2 diabetes is generally treated with medication that is taken by mouth (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Metformin" title="Metformin">metformin</a>) although some eventually require injectable treatment with insulin or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GLP-1_agonist" title="GLP-1 agonist">GLP-1 agonists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICENG28_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICENG28-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Metformin" title="Metformin">Metformin</a> is generally recommended as a first-line treatment for type 2 diabetes, as there is good evidence that it decreases mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP09_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-AFP09-9">[9]</a></sup> It works by decreasing the liver's production of glucose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugs2005_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drugs2005-123">[123]</a></sup> Several other groups of drugs, mostly given by mouth, may also decrease blood sugar in type 2 diabetes. These include agents that increase insulin release (<a href="/wiki/Sulfonylurea" title="Sulfonylurea">sulfonylureas</a>), agents that decrease absorption of sugar from the intestines (<a href="/wiki/Acarbose" title="Acarbose">acarbose</a>), agents that inhibit the enzyme dipeptidyl peptidase-4 (DPP-4) that inactivates incretins such as GLP-1 and GIP (<a href="/wiki/Sitagliptin" title="Sitagliptin">sitagliptin</a>), agents that make the body more sensitive to insulin (<a href="/wiki/Thiazolidinedione" title="Thiazolidinedione">thiazolidinedione</a>) and agents that increase the excretion of glucose in the urine (<a href="/wiki/SGLT2_inhibitor" title="SGLT2 inhibitor">SGLT2 inhibitors</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugs2005_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Drugs2005-123">[123]</a></sup> When insulin is used in type 2 diabetes, a long-acting formulation is usually added initially, while continuing oral medications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP09_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-AFP09-9">[9]</a></sup> Doses of insulin are then increased until glucose targets are reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP09_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-AFP09-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_pressure_lowering">Blood pressure lowering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Blood pressure lowering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">Cardiovascular disease</a> is a serious complication associated with diabetes, and many international guidelines recommend blood pressure treatment targets that are lower than 140/90 mmHg for people with diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> However, there is only limited evidence regarding what the lower targets should be. A 2016 systematic review found potential harm to treating to targets lower than 140 mmHg,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and a subsequent systematic review in 2019 found no evidence of additional benefit from blood pressure lowering to between 130 – 140mmHg, although there was an increased risk of adverse events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>2015 American Diabetes Association recommendations are that people with diabetes and albuminuria should receive an inhibitor of the renin-angiotensin system to reduce the risks of progression to end-stage renal disease, cardiovascular events, and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-128">[128]</a></sup> There is some evidence that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiotensin_converting_enzyme_inhibitors" title="Angiotensin converting enzyme inhibitors">angiotensin converting enzyme inhibitors</a> (ACEIs) are superior to other inhibitors of the renin-angiotensin system such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiotensin_receptor_blockers" title="Angiotensin receptor blockers">angiotensin receptor blockers</a> (ARBs),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Aliskiren" title="Aliskiren">aliskiren</a> in preventing cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Although a more recent review found similar effects of ACEIs and ARBs on major cardiovascular and renal outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-131">[131]</a></sup> There is no evidence that combining ACEIs and ARBs provides additional benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aspirin">Aspirin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Aspirin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a> to prevent cardiovascular disease in diabetes is controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-128">[128]</a></sup> Aspirin is recommended by some in people at high risk of cardiovascular disease, however routine use of aspirin has not been found to improve outcomes in uncomplicated diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> 2015 American Diabetes Association recommendations for aspirin use (based on expert consensus or clinical experience) are that low-dose aspirin use is reasonable in adults with diabetes who are at intermediate risk of cardiovascular disease (10-year cardiovascular disease risk, 5–10%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-128">[128]</a></sup> National guidelines for England and Wales by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Health_and_Care_Excellence" title="National Institute for Health and Care Excellence">National Institute for Health and Care Excellence</a> (NICE) recommend against the use of aspirin in people with type 1 or type 2 diabetes who do not have confirmed cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICENG17_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-NICENG17-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICENG28_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-NICENG28-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bariatric_surgery" title="Bariatric surgery">Weight loss surgery</a> in those with <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> and type 2 diabetes is often an effective measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Picot2009_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Picot2009-23">[23]</a></sup> Many are able to maintain normal blood sugar levels with little or no medications following surgery<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> and long-term mortality is decreased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schum2009_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schum2009-134">[134]</a></sup> There is, however, a short-term mortality risk of less than 1% from the surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> cutoffs for when surgery is appropriate are not yet clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schum2009_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schum2009-134">[134]</a></sup> It is recommended that this option be considered in those who are unable to get both their weight and blood sugar under control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pancreas_transplant" title="Pancreas transplant">pancreas transplant</a> is occasionally considered for people with type 1 diabetes who have severe complications of their disease, including <a href="/wiki/Chronic_kidney_disease" title="Chronic kidney disease">end stage kidney disease</a> requiring <a href="/wiki/Kidney_transplantation" title="Kidney transplantation">kidney transplantation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-management_and_support">Self-management and support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Self-management and support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In countries using a <a href="/wiki/General_practitioner" title="General practitioner">general practitioner</a> system, such as the United Kingdom, care may take place mainly outside hospitals, with hospital-based specialist care used only in case of complications, difficult blood sugar control, or research projects. In other circumstances, general practitioners and specialists share care in a team approach. Home <a href="/wiki/Telehealth" title="Telehealth">telehealth</a> support can be an effective management technique.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polisena_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polisena-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> to deliver educational programs for adults with type 2 diabetes includes computer-based self-management interventions to collect for tailored responses to facilitate self-management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-pal-139">[139]</a></sup> There is no adequate evidence to support effects on <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Behavior_change_(public_health)" title="Behavior change (public health)">behavioral change</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a> levels and dietary), <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>, weight and <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life_(healthcare)" title="Quality of life (healthcare)">health-related quality of life</a>, nor in other biological, cognitive or emotional outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-pal-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology_of_diabetes" title="Epidemiology of diabetes">Epidemiology of diabetes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prevalence_of_Diabetes_by_Percent_of_Country_Population_(2014)_Gradient_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="862" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Prevalence_of_Diabetes_by_Percent_of_Country_Population_%282014%29_Gradient_Map.png/310px-Prevalence_of_Diabetes_by_Percent_of_Country_Population_%282014%29_Gradient_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Prevalence_of_Diabetes_by_Percent_of_Country_Population_%282014%29_Gradient_Map.png/465px-Prevalence_of_Diabetes_by_Percent_of_Country_Population_%282014%29_Gradient_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Prevalence_of_Diabetes_by_Percent_of_Country_Population_%282014%29_Gradient_Map.png/620px-Prevalence_of_Diabetes_by_Percent_of_Country_Population_%282014%29_Gradient_Map.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prevalence_of_Diabetes_by_Percent_of_Country_Population_(2014)_Gradient_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rates of diabetes worldwide in 2014. title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mortality rate of diabetes worldwide in 2012 per million inhabitants <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin refbegin-columns references-column-count references-column-count-3" style="column-count: 3;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px color:black;"> </span> 28–91</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffe820; color:black;"> </span> 92–114</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffd820; color:black;"> </span> 115–141</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc020; color:black;"> </span> 142–163</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffa020; color:black;"> </span> 164–184</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a20; color:black;"> </span> 185–209</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f08015; color:black;"> </span> 210–247</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e06815; color:black;"> </span> 248–309</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d85010; color:black;"> </span> 310–404</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d02010; color:white;"> </span> 405–1879</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In 2017, 425 million people had diabetes worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2017_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2017-141">[141]</a></sup> up from an estimated 382 million people in 2013<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shi2014_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shi2014-142">[142]</a></sup> and from 108 million in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-143">[143]</a></sup> Accounting for the shifting age structure of the global population, the prevalence of diabetes is 8.8% among adults, nearly double the rate of 4.7% in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2017_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2017-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-143">[143]</a></sup> Type 2 makes up about 90% of the cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vos2012_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vos2012-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2011_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Will2011-46">[46]</a></sup> Some data indicate rates are roughly equal in women and men,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vos2012_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vos2012-25">[25]</a></sup> but male excess in diabetes has been found in many populations with higher type 2 incidence, possibly due to sex-related differences in insulin sensitivity, consequences of obesity and regional body fat deposition, and other contributing factors such as high blood pressure, tobacco smoking, and alcohol intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>The WHO estimates that diabetes resulted in 1.5 million deaths in 2012, making it the 8th leading cause of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013Top10_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013Top10-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-143">[143]</a></sup> However another 2.2 million deaths worldwide were attributable to high blood glucose and the increased risks of cardiovascular disease and other associated complications (e.g. kidney failure), which often lead to premature death and are often listed as the underlying cause on death certificates rather than diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_143-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> For example, in 2017, the <a href="/wiki/International_Diabetes_Federation" title="International Diabetes Federation">International Diabetes Federation</a> (IDF) estimated that diabetes resulted in 4.0 million deaths worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2017_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2017-141">[141]</a></sup> using modeling to estimate the total number of deaths that could be directly or indirectly attributed to diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2017_141-3"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2017-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diabetes occurs throughout the world but is more common (especially type 2) in more developed countries. The greatest increase in rates has however been seen in low- and middle-income countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_143-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-143">[143]</a></sup> where more than 80% of diabetic deaths occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The fastest prevalence increase is expected to occur in Asia and Africa, where most people with diabetes will probably live in 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wild2004_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wild2004-148">[148]</a></sup> The increase in rates in developing countries follows the trend of urbanization and lifestyle changes, including increasingly sedentary lifestyles, less physically demanding work and the global nutrition transition, marked by increased intake of foods that are high energy-dense but nutrient-poor (often high in sugar and saturated fats, sometimes referred to as the "Western-style" diet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_143-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wild2004_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wild2004-148">[148]</a></sup> The global number of diabetes cases might increase by 48% between 2017 and 2045.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDF2017_141-4"><a href="#cite_note-IDF2017-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_diabetes" title="History of diabetes">History of diabetes</a></div>
<p>Diabetes was one of the first diseases described,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> with an <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> manuscript from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a> mentioning "too great emptying of the urine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History2010_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-History2010-150">[150]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebers_papyrus" title="Ebers papyrus">Ebers papyrus</a> includes a recommendation for a drink to take in such cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-151">[151]</a></sup> The first described cases are believed to have been type 1 diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History2010_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-History2010-150">[150]</a></sup> Indian physicians around the same time identified the disease and classified it as <i>madhumeha</i> or "honey urine", noting the urine would attract ants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History2010_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-History2010-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "diabetes" or "to pass through" was first used in 230 BCE by the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollonius_(physician)" title="Apollonius (physician)">Apollonius of Memphis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History2010_150-3"><a href="#cite_note-History2010-150">[150]</a></sup> The disease was considered rare during the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_empire" title="Roman empire">Roman empire</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> commenting he had only seen two cases during his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History2010_150-4"><a href="#cite_note-History2010-150">[150]</a></sup> This is possibly due to the diet and lifestyle of the ancients, or because the clinical symptoms were observed during the advanced stage of the disease. Galen named the disease "diarrhea of the urine" (diarrhea urinosa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laios_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laios-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest surviving work with a detailed reference to diabetes is that of <a href="/wiki/Aretaeus_of_Cappadocia" title="Aretaeus of Cappadocia">Aretaeus of Cappadocia</a> (2nd or early 3rd<span class="nowrap"> </span>century CE). He described the symptoms and the course of the disease, which he attributed to the moisture and coldness, reflecting the beliefs of the "<a href="/wiki/Pneumatic_school" title="Pneumatic school">Pneumatic School</a>". He hypothesized a correlation between diabetes and other diseases, and he discussed differential diagnosis from the snakebite, which also provokes excessive thirst. His work remained unknown in the West until 1552, when the first Latin edition was published in Venice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laios_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Laios-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two types of diabetes were identified as separate conditions for the first time by the Indian physicians <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Sushruta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charaka" title="Charaka">Charaka</a> in 400–500 CE with one type being associated with youth and another type with being overweight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History2010_150-5"><a href="#cite_note-History2010-150">[150]</a></sup> Effective treatment was not developed until the early part of the 20th century when Canadians <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Banting" title="Frederick Banting">Frederick Banting</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Herbert_Best" title="Charles Herbert Best">Charles Herbert Best</a> isolated and purified insulin in 1921 and 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History2010_150-6"><a href="#cite_note-History2010-150">[150]</a></sup> This was followed by the development of the long-acting insulin <a href="/wiki/NPH_insulin" title="NPH insulin">NPH</a> in the 1940s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History2010_150-7"><a href="#cite_note-History2010-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word <i>diabetes</i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>) comes from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>diabētēs</i>, which in turn comes from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> διαβήτης (<i>diabētēs</i>), which literally means "a passer through; a <a href="/wiki/Siphon" title="Siphon">siphon</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_diabetes_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED_diabetes-153">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> <a href="/wiki/Aretaeus_of_Cappadocia" title="Aretaeus of Cappadocia">Aretaeus of Cappadocia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">fl.</a> 1st<span class="nowrap"> </span>century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>) used that word, with the intended meaning "excessive discharge of urine", as the name for the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtymology_diabetes_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtymology_diabetes-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Ultimately, the word comes from Greek διαβαίνειν (<i>diabainein</i>), meaning "to pass through",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_diabetes_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED_diabetes-153">[153]</a></sup> which is composed of δια- (<i>dia</i>-), meaning "through" and βαίνειν (<i>bainein</i>), meaning "to go".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtymology_diabetes_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtymology_diabetes-154">[154]</a></sup> The word "diabetes" is first recorded in English, in the form <i>diabete</i>, in a medical text written around 1425.
</p><p>The word <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mellitus" title="wikt:mellitus">mellitus</a></i> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>) comes from the classical Latin word <i>mellītus</i>, meaning "mellite"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_mellite_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED_mellite-156">[156]</a></sup> (i.e. sweetened with honey;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_mellite_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED_mellite-156">[156]</a></sup> honey-sweet<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MyEtymology_mellitus_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-MyEtymology_mellitus-157">[157]</a></sup>). The Latin word comes from <i>mell</i>-, which comes from <i>mel</i>, meaning "honey";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_mellite_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-OED_mellite-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MyEtymology_mellitus_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-MyEtymology_mellitus-157">[157]</a></sup> sweetness;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MyEtymology_mellitus_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-MyEtymology_mellitus-157">[157]</a></sup> pleasant thing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MyEtymology_mellitus_157-3"><a href="#cite_note-MyEtymology_mellitus-157">[157]</a></sup> and the suffix -<i>ītus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_mellite_156-3"><a href="#cite_note-OED_mellite-156">[156]</a></sup> whose meaning is the same as that of the English suffix "-ite".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_-ite_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED_-ite-158">[158]</a></sup> It was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Willis" title="Thomas Willis">Thomas Willis</a> who in 1675 added "mellitus" to the word "diabetes" as a designation for the disease, when he noticed the urine of a person with diabetes had a sweet taste (glycosuria). This sweet taste had been noticed in urine by the ancient Greeks, Chinese, Egyptians, Indians, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_films_featuring_diabetes" title="List of films featuring diabetes">List of films featuring diabetes</a></div>
<p>The 1989 "<a href="/wiki/St._Vincent_Declaration" title="St. Vincent Declaration">St. Vincent Declaration</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> was the result of international efforts to improve the care accorded to those with diabetes. Doing so is important not only in terms of quality of life and life expectancy but also economically – expenses due to diabetes have been shown to be a major drain on health – and productivity-related resources for healthcare systems and governments.
</p><p>Several countries established more and less successful national diabetes programmes to improve treatment of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EO005-Dubois&amp;Bankauskaite_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-EO005-Dubois&amp;Bankauskaite-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>People with diabetes who have neuropathic symptoms such as numbness or tingling in feet or hands are twice as likely to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unemployed" title="Unemployed">unemployed</a> as those without the symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17563611_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17563611-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, diabetes-related emergency room (ER) visit rates in the United States were higher among people from the lowest income communities (526 per 10,000 population) than from the highest income communities (236 per 10,000 population). Approximately 9.4% of diabetes-related ER visits were for the uninsured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hcup-us.ahrq_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-hcup-us.ahrq-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term "type 1 diabetes" has replaced several former terms, including childhood-onset diabetes, juvenile diabetes, and insulin-dependent diabetes mellitus. Likewise, the term "type 2 diabetes" has replaced several former terms, including adult-onset diabetes, obesity-related diabetes, and noninsulin-dependent diabetes mellitus. Beyond these two types, there is no agreed-upon standard nomenclature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diabetes mellitus is also occasionally known as "sugar diabetes" to differentiate it from <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_insipidus" title="Diabetes insipidus">diabetes insipidus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_in_dogs" title="Diabetes in dogs">Diabetes in dogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_in_cats" title="Diabetes in cats">Diabetes in cats</a></div>
<p>In animals, diabetes is most commonly encountered in dogs and cats. Middle-aged animals are most commonly affected. Female dogs are twice as likely to be affected as males, while according to some sources, male cats are more prone than females. In both species, all breeds may be affected, but some small dog breeds are particularly likely to develop diabetes, such as <a href="/wiki/Poodle" title="Poodle">Miniature Poodles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merck-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>Feline diabetes is strikingly similar to human type 2 diabetes. The <a href="/wiki/Burmese_cat" title="Burmese cat">Burmese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Blue" title="Russian Blue">Russian Blue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abyssinian_cat" title="Abyssinian cat">Abyssinian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Forest_cat" title="Norwegian Forest cat">Norwegian Forest</a> cat breeds are at higher risk than other breeds. Overweight cats are also at higher risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>The symptoms may relate to fluid loss and polyuria, but the course may also be insidious. Diabetic animals are more prone to infections. The long-term complications recognized in humans are much rarer in animals. The principles of treatment (weight loss, oral antidiabetics, subcutaneous insulin) and management of emergencies (e.g. ketoacidosis) are similar to those in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merck-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diabetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
